there is an agreement on educational cooperation between the Faroe Islands and Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cultral_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-cultral-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adult_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-adult-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In 2012 the public spending on education was 8.1% of <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-fin-58">[58]</a></sup> The municipalities are responsible for the school buildings for children's education in Fólkaskúlin from age 1st grade to 9th or 10th grade (age 7 to 16).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sk.C3.BAlavi.C3.B0urskifti_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sk.C3.BAlavi.C3.B0urskifti-59">[59]</a></sup> In November 2013 1,615 people, or 6.8% of the total number of employees, were employed in the education sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-fin-58">[58]</a></sup> Of the 31,270 people aged 25 and above 1,717 (5.5%) have gained at least a <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degrees</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a>, 8,428 (27%) have gained a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science" title="Bachelor of Science">B.Sc.</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Diploma" title="Diploma">diploma</a>, 11,706 (37.4%) have finished upper secondary education while 9,419 (30.1%) has only finished primary school and have no other education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> There is no data on literacy in the Faroe Islands, but the CIA Factbook states that it is probably as high as in <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> proper, i.e. 99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority of students in upper secondary schools are women, although men represent the majority in higher education institutions. In addition, most young Faroese people who relocate to other countries to study are women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Studni2015_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Studni2015-62">[62]</a></sup> Out of 8,535 holders of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bachelor_degrees" title="Bachelor degrees">bachelor degrees</a>, 4,796 (56.2%) have had their education in the Faroe Islands, 2,724 (31.9%) in Denmark, 543 in both the Faroe Islands and Denmark, 94 (1.1%) in Norway, 80 in the United Kingdom and the rest in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-educationpercountry_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-educationpercountry-63">[63]</a></sup> Out of 1,719 holders of master's degrees or PhDs, 1,249 (72.7% have had their education in Denmark, 87 (5.1%) in the United Kingdom, 86 (5%) in both the Faroe Islands and Denmark, 64 (3.7%) in the Faroe Islands, 60 (3.5%) in Norway and the rest in other countries (mostly EU and Nordic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-educationpercountry_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-educationpercountry-63">[63]</a></sup> Since there is no medical school in the Faroe Islands, all medical students have to study abroad; as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, out of a total of 96 medical students, 76 studied in Denmark, 19 in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and 1 in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L.C3.A6knalesandi_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-L.C3.A6knalesandi-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Economy of the Faroe Islands">Economy of the Faroe Islands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="863" data-file-width="1104" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg/220px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg/330px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg/440px-Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tree_map_exports_2009_Faeore_Islands.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graphical depiction of Faroe Islands' product exports in 28 colour-coded categories</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Economic troubles caused by a collapse of the Faroese fishing industry in the early 1990s brought high unemployment rates of 10 to 15% by the mid-1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatsFO_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-StatsFO-65">[65]</a></sup> Unemployment decreased in the later 1990s, down to about 6% at the end of 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatsFO_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-StatsFO-65">[65]</a></sup> By June 2008 unemployment had declined to 1.1%, before rising to 3.4% in early 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StatsFO_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-StatsFO-65">[65]</a></sup> In December 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagstovan_unemployment_2015_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hagstovan_unemployment_2015-66">[66]</a></sup> the unemployment was 3.2%. Nevertheless, the almost total dependence on fishing and <a href="/wiki/Fish_farming" title="Fish farming">fish farming</a> means that the economy remains vulnerable. One of the biggest private companies of the Faroe Islands is the salmon farming company <a href="/wiki/Bakkafrost" title="Bakkafrost">Bakkafrost</a>, which is the largest of the four salmon farming companies in the Faroe Islands<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fishupdate_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fishupdate-67">[67]</a></sup> and the eighth biggest in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_Islands,_Bor%C3%B0oy,_Klaksv%C3%ADk_(3).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="2144" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg/220px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg/330px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg/440px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Bor%C3%B0oy%2C_Klaksv%C3%ADk_%283%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_Islands,_Bor%C3%B0oy,_Klaksv%C3%ADk_(3).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Klaksv%C3%ADk" title="Klaksvík">Klaksvík</a>, on the island of Borðoy, is the Faroe Islands' second-largest town.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Petroleum found close to the Faroese area gives hope for deposits in the immediate area, which may provide a basis for sustained economic prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>13% of the Faroe Islands' national income comes as economic aid from <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> This corresponds to roughly 5% of GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 2000, the government has fostered new information technology and business projects to attract new investment. The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Burger_King" title="Burger King">Burger King</a> in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B3rshavn" title="Tórshavn">Tórshavn</a> was widely publicized as a sign of the globalization of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Culture of the Faroe Islands">Faroese culture</a>. It remains to be seen whether these projects will succeed in broadening the islands' economic base. The islands have one of the lowest unemployment rates in Europe, but this should not necessarily be taken as a sign of a recovering economy, as many young students move to Denmark and other countries after leaving high school. This leaves a largely middle-aged and elderly population that may lack the skills and knowledge to fill newly developed positions on the Faroes. Nonetheless, in 2008 the Faroes were able to make a $52 million loan to <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> to help with that country's banking woes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 August 2009, two opposition parties introduced a bill in the <i><a href="/wiki/L%C3%B8gting" title="Løgting">Løgting</a></i> to adopt the euro as the national currency, pending a referendum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Transport in the Faroe Islands">Transport in the Faroe Islands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_Islands,_Eysturoy,_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="997" data-file-width="1501" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg/220px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg/330px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg/440px-Faroe_Islands%2C_Eysturoy%2C_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_Islands,_Eysturoy,_road_from_Skipanes_to_Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The road network on the Faroe Islands is highly developed. Shown here is the road from <a href="/wiki/Skipanes" title="Skipanes">Skipanes</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sy%C3%B0rug%C3%B8ta" title="Syðrugøta">Syðrugøta</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Eysturoy" title="Eysturoy">Eysturoy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By road, the main islands are connected by bridges and tunnels. Government owned <a href="/wiki/Strandfaraskip_Landsins" title="Strandfaraskip Landsins">Strandfaraskip Landsins</a> provides public bus and ferry service to the main towns and villages. There are no railways.</p>
<p>By air, the government owned <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Airways" title="Atlantic Airways">Atlantic Airways</a> provides helicopter service to each of the islands. Atlantic Airways and other airlines have scheduled international flights to <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1gar_Airport" title="Vágar Airport">Vágar Airport</a>, the islands' only airport. All civil aviation matters are controlled from the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Administration_Denmark" title="Civil Aviation Administration Denmark">Civil Aviation Administration Denmark</a>.</p>
<p>By sea, the <a href="/wiki/Smyril_Line" title="Smyril Line">Smyril Line</a> operates a regular international passenger, car and freight service linking the Faroe Islands with <a href="/wiki/Sey%C3%B0isfj%C3%B6r%C3%B0ur" title="Seyðisfjörður">Seyðisfjörður</a>, Iceland and <a href="/wiki/Hirtshals" title="Hirtshals">Hirtshals</a>, Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_smyril_11.56.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/New_smyril_11.56.jpg/220px-New_smyril_11.56.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/New_smyril_11.56.jpg/330px-New_smyril_11.56.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/New_smyril_11.56.jpg/440px-New_smyril_11.56.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_smyril_11.56.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new ferry <a href="/wiki/MS_Smyril" title="MS Smyril">MS <i>Smyril</i></a> enters the Faroe Islands at <a href="/wiki/Krambatangi" title="Krambatangi">Krambatangi</a> ferry port in <a href="/wiki/Su%C3%B0uroy" title="Suðuroy">Suðuroy</a>, 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of the rocky terrain in the Faroe Islands, its road transport system was not<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2017)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> as extensive as in other places of the world. This situation has now changed, and the infrastructure has been developed extensively. Some 80 percent of the population of the islands is connected by <a href="/wiki/List_of_tunnels_of_the_Faroe_Islands" title="List of tunnels of the Faroe Islands">tunnels through the mountains and between the islands</a>, bridges and <a href="/wiki/Causeway" title="Causeway">causeways</a> that link the three largest islands and three other larger and smaller islands to the northeast together. While the other two large islands to the south of the main area, Sandoy and Suðuroy, are connected to the main area with <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferries</a>, the small islands Koltur and Stóra Dímun have no ferry connection, only helicopter service. Other small islands—Mykines in the west, Kalsoy, Svínoy and Fugloy in the north, Hestur west of Streymoy, and Nólsoy east of Tórshavn—have smaller ferries and some of these islands even have helicopter service. In February 2014 all the political parties of the <i>Løgting</i> agreed on making two subsea tunnels, one between Streymoy and Eysturoy (the <a href="/wiki/Runav%C3%ADk#Eysturoyartunnilin" title="Runavík">Eysturoyartunnilin</a>) and one between Streymoy and Sandoy (<a href="/wiki/Sandoyartunnilin" title="Sandoyartunnilin">Sandoyartunnilin</a>). The plan is that both tunnels should open in 2021 and they will not be private.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The work to dig the Eysturoy-tunnel started on 1 March 2016 above the village of <a href="/wiki/Hv%C3%ADtanes" title="Hvítanes">Hvítanes</a> near Tórshavn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grabbin_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grabbin-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Culture of the Faroe Islands">Culture of the Faroe Islands</a></div>
<p>The culture of the Faroe Islands has its roots in the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic</a> culture. The Faroe Islands were long isolated from the main cultural phases and movements that swept across parts of Europe. This means that they have maintained a great part of their traditional culture. The language spoken is <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a> and it is one of three insular <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">North Germanic languages</a> descended from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> language spoken in Scandinavia in the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a>, the others being <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a>, which is thought to have been mutually intelligible with Faroese. Until the 15th century, Faroese had a similar orthography to Icelandic and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, but after the Reformation in 1538, the ruling <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegians</a> outlawed its use in schools, churches and official documents. Although a rich spoken tradition survived, for 300 years the language was not written down. This means that all poems and stories were handed down orally. These works were split into the following divisions: sagnir (historical), ævintýr (stories) and <a href="/wiki/Kv%C3%A6%C3%B0i" title="Kvæði">kvæði</a> (ballads), often set to music and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> chain dance. These were eventually written down in the 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faroese_literature">Faroese literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Faroese literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faroese_literature" title="Faroese literature">Faroese literature</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:207px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_364_rasmussen_and_skardi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="205" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6f/Faroe_stamp_364_rasmussen_and_skardi.jpg" width="205"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroe_stamp_364_rasmussen_and_skardi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regin_%C3%AD_L%C3%AD%C3%B0" title="Regin í Líð">Rasmus Rasmussen</a>, the writer who wrote the first novel in the <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese language</a> (poetical name: Regin í Líð) and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%ADmun_av_Skar%C3%B0i" title="Símun av Skarði">Símun av Skarði</a>, the poet who wrote the Faroese national hymn</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Faroese written literature has only really developed in the past 100–200 years. This is mainly because of the islands' isolation, and also because the <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese language</a> was not written down in a standardised format until 1890. The Danish language was also encouraged at the expense of Faroese. Nevertheless, the Faroes have produced several authors and poets. A rich centuries-old oral tradition of folk tales and Faroese folk songs accompanied the <a href="/wiki/Faroese_dance" title="Faroese dance">Faroese chain dance</a>. The people learned these songs and stories by heart, and told or sung them to each other, teaching the younger generations too. This kind of literature was gathered in the 19th century and early 20th century. The Faroese folk songs, in Faroese called <i>kvæði</i>, are still in use although not so large-scale as earlier. Some of the Faroese folk songs have been used by the Faroese Viking metal band <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr_(band)" title="Týr (band)">Týr</a>, i.e., <i><a href="/wiki/Ormurin_Langi" title="Ormurin Langi">Ormurin Langi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/List_of_novels_written_in_Faroese" title="List of novels written in Faroese">Faroese novel</a>, <i>Bábelstornið</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regin_%C3%AD_L%C3%AD%C3%B0" title="Regin í Líð">Regin í Líð</a>, was published in 1909; the second novel was published 18 years later. In the period 1930 to 1940 a writer from the village Skálavík on Sandoy island, <a href="/wiki/He%C3%B0in_Br%C3%BA" title="Heðin Brú">Heðin Brú</a>, published three novels: <i>Lognbrá</i> (1930), <i>Fastatøkur</i> (1935) and <i>Feðgar á ferð</i> (English title: <i>The old man and his sons</i>) (1940). <i>Feðgar á ferð</i> has been translated into several other languages. Martin Joensen from <a href="/wiki/Sandv%C3%ADk" title="Sandvík">Sandvík</a> wrote about life on Faroese fishing vessels; he published the novels <i>Fiskimenn</i> (1946)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> and <i>Tað lýsir á landi</i> (1952).</p>
<p>Well-known poets from the early 20th century are among others the two brothers from Tórshavn: <a href="/wiki/Hans_Andrias_Djurhuus" title="Hans Andrias Djurhuus">Hans Andrias Djurhuus</a> (1883–1951)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Janus_Djurhuus" title="Janus Djurhuus">Janus Djurhuus</a> (1881–1948),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> other well known poets from this period and the mid 20th century are <a href="/wiki/Poul_F._Joensen" title="Poul F. Joensen">Poul F. Joensen</a> (1898–1970),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Regin_Dahl" title="Regin Dahl">Regin Dahl</a> (1918–2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and Tummas Napoleon Djurhuus (1928–71).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Their poems are popular even today and can be found in Faroese song books and school books. <a href="/wiki/Jens_Pauli_Heinesen" title="Jens Pauli Heinesen">Jens Pauli Heinesen</a> (1932–2011), a school teacher from <a href="/wiki/Sandav%C3%A1gur" title="Sandavágur">Sandavágur</a>, was the most productive Faroese novelist, he published 17 novels. <a href="/wiki/Steinbj%C3%B8rn_B._Jacobsen" title="Steinbjørn B. Jacobsen">Steinbjørn B. Jacobsen</a> (1937–2012), a schoolteacher from <a href="/wiki/Sandv%C3%ADk" title="Sandvík">Sandvík</a>, wrote short stories, plays, children's books and even novels. Most Faroese writers write in Faroese; two exceptions are <a href="/wiki/William_Heinesen" title="William Heinesen">William Heinesen</a> (1900–91) and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B8rgen-Frantz_Jacobsen" title="Jørgen-Frantz Jacobsen">Jørgen-Frantz Jacobsen</a> (1900–38).</p>
<p>Women were not so visible in the early Faroese literature except for <a href="/wiki/Helena_Patursson" title="Helena Patursson">Helena Patursson</a> (1864–1916), but in the last decades of the 20th century and in the beginning of the 21st century female writers like <a href="/wiki/Ebba_Hentze" title="Ebba Hentze">Ebba Hentze</a> (born 1933) wrote children's books, short stories, etc. <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%B0ri%C3%B0_Helmsdal" title="Guðrið Helmsdal">Guðrið Helmsdal</a> published the first modernistic collection of poems, <i>Lýtt lot</i>, in 1963, which at the same time was the first collection of Faroese poems written by a woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Her daughter, <a href="/wiki/Rakel_Helmsdal" title="Rakel Helmsdal">Rakel Helmsdal</a> (born 1966), is also a writer, best known for her children's books, for which she has won several prizes and nominations. Other female writers are the novelists <a href="/wiki/Oddv%C3%B8r_Johansen" title="Oddvør Johansen">Oddvør Johansen</a> (born 1941), <a href="/wiki/Bergt%C3%B3ra_Hanusard%C3%B3ttir" title="Bergtóra Hanusardóttir">Bergtóra Hanusardóttir</a> (born 1946) and novelist/children's books writers <a href="/wiki/Marianna_Debes_Dahl" title="Marianna Debes Dahl">Marianna Debes Dahl</a> (born 1947), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%B3lrun_Michelsen" title="Sólrun Michelsen">Sólrun Michelsen</a> (born 1948). Other modern Faroese writers include Gunnar Hoydal (born 1941), <a href="/wiki/Hanus_Kamban" title="Hanus Kamban">Hanus Kamban</a> (born 1942), <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3gvan_Isaksen" title="Jógvan Isaksen">Jógvan Isaksen</a> (born 1950), <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3anes_Nielsen" title="Jóanes Nielsen">Jóanes Nielsen</a> (born 1953), Tóroddur Poulsen and <a href="/wiki/Carl_J%C3%B3han_Jensen" title="Carl Jóhan Jensen">Carl Jóhan Jensen</a> (born 1957). Some of these writers have been nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council%27s_Literature_Prize" title="Nordic Council's Literature Prize">Nordic Council's Literature Prize</a> two to six times, but have never won it. The only Faroese writer who writes in Faroese who has won the prize is the poet <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B3i_Patursson" title="Rói Patursson">Rói Patursson</a> (born 1947), who won the prize in 1986 for <i>Líkasum</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 21st century some new writers had success in the Faroe Islands and abroad. <a href="/wiki/B%C3%A1r%C3%B0ur_Oskarsson" title="Bárður Oskarsson">Bárður Oskarsson</a> (born 1972) is a children's book writer and illustrator; his books won prizes in the Faroes, Germany and the <a href="/wiki/West_Nordic_Council%27s_Children_and_Youth_Literature_Prize" title="West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize">West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize</a> (2006). Though not born in the Faroe Islands, Matthew Landrum an American poet and editor for the Structo magazine, has written a collection of poems about the Islands. <a href="/wiki/Sissal_Kampmann" title="Sissal Kampmann">Sissal Kampmann</a> (born 1974) won the Danish literary prize <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Rifbjerg%27s_Debutant_Prize" title="Klaus Rifbjerg's Debutant Prize">Klaus Rifbjerg's Debutant Prize</a> (2012), and Rakel Helmsdal has won Faroese and Icelandic awards; she has been nominated for the <a href="/wiki/West_Nordic_Council%27s_Children_and_Youth_Literature_Prize" title="West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize">West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize</a> and the Children and Youth Literature Prize of the Nordic Council (representing Iceland, wrote the book together with and Icelandic and a Swedish writer/illustrator). <a href="/wiki/Marjun_Syderb%C3%B8_Kjeln%C3%A6s" title="Marjun Syderbø Kjelnæs">Marjun Syderbø Kjelnæs</a> (born 1974) had success with her first novel <i>Skriva í sandin</i> for teenagers; the book was awarded and nominated both in the Faroes and in other countries. She won the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Children%27s_Book_Prize" title="Nordic Children's Book Prize">Nordic Children's Book Prize</a> (2011) for this book, <a href="/wiki/International_Youth_Library" title="International Youth Library">White Raven Deutsche Jugendbibliothek</a> (2011) and nominated the <a href="/wiki/West_Nordic_Council%27s_Children_and_Youth_Literature_Prize" title="West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize">West Nordic Council's Children and Youth Literature Prize</a> and the Children and Youth Literature Prize of the Nordic Council (2013).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Music of the Faroe Islands">Music of the Faroe Islands</a></div>
<p>The Faroe Islands have an active music scene, with live music being a regular part of the Islands' life and many Faroese being proficient at a number of instruments. Multiple Danish Music Award winner <a href="/wiki/Teitur_Lassen" title="Teitur Lassen">Teitur Lassen</a> calls the Faroes home and is arguably the Islands' most internationally well-known musical export.</p>
<p>The Islands have their own <a href="/wiki/Orchestra" title="Orchestra">orchestra</a> (the classical ensemble <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Aldub%C3%A1ran&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Aldubáran (page does not exist)">Aldubáran</a></i>) and many different choirs; the best-known of these is <i>Havnarkórið</i>. The best-known local Faroese composers are <a href="/wiki/Sunleif_Rasmussen" title="Sunleif Rasmussen">Sunleif Rasmussen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kristian_Blak" title="Kristian Blak">Kristian Blak</a>, who is also head of the record company <a href="/wiki/Tutl" title="Tutl">Tutl</a>. The first Faroese opera was by Sunleif Rasmussen. It is entitled <i>Í Óðamansgarði</i> (The Madman's Garden) and was premiered on 12 October 2006 at the Nordic House. The opera is based on a short story by the writer <a href="/wiki/William_Heinesen" title="William Heinesen">William Heinesen</a>.</p>
<p>Young Faroese musicians who have gained much popularity recently are <a href="/wiki/Eiv%C3%B8r_P%C3%A1lsd%C3%B3ttir" title="Eivør Pálsdóttir">Eivør Pálsdóttir</a>, Anna Katrin Egilstrøð, Lena (Lena Andersen), <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B8gni_Reistrup" title="Høgni Reistrup">Høgni Reistrup</a>, <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B8gni_Lisberg" title="Høgni Lisberg">Høgni Lisberg</a>, HEIÐRIK (<a href="/wiki/Hei%C3%B0rik_%C3%A1_Heygum" title="Heiðrik á Heygum">Heiðrik á Heygum</a>), <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%B0ri%C3%B0_Hansd%C3%B3ttir" title="Guðrið Hansdóttir">Guðrið Hansdóttir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brandur_Enni" title="Brandur Enni">Brandur Enni</a>.</p>
<p>Well-known bands include <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr_(band)" title="Týr (band)">Týr</a>, Gestir, <a href="/wiki/Hamfer%C3%B0" title="Hamferð">Hamferð</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Ghost_(Faroese_band)" title="The Ghost (Faroese band)">The Ghost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boys_in_a_Band" title="Boys in a Band">Boys in a Band</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ORKA&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ORKA (page does not exist)">ORKA</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://fo.wikipedia.org/wiki/200_(rokkb%C3%B3lkur)" title="fo:200 (rokkbólkur)">200</a>, Grandma's Basement, <a href="/wiki/Sic_(band)" title="Sic (band)">SIC</a>, and the former band Clickhaze.</p>
<p>The festival of contemporary and classical music, <i>Summartónar</i>, is held each summer. The <a href="/wiki/G!_Festival" title="G! Festival">G! Festival</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nor%C3%B0rag%C3%B8ta" title="Norðragøta">Norðragøta</a> in July and <a href="/wiki/Summarfestivalurin" title="Summarfestivalurin">Summarfestivalurin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Klaksv%C3%ADk" title="Klaksvík">Klaksvík</a> in August are both large, open-air music festivals for popular music with both local and international musicians participating. The world renowned Zappa Jazz Festival will be held August 2016.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Nordic_House_in_the_Faroe_Islands">The Nordic House in the Faroe Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The Nordic House in the Faroe Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nordic_House_in_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Nordic House in the Faroe Islands">Nordic House in the Faroe Islands</a> (<a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>: <span lang="fo" xml:lang="fo"><i>Norðurlandahúsið</i></span>) is the most important cultural institution in the Faroes. Its aim is to support and promote <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> and Faroese culture, locally and in the Nordic region. <a href="/wiki/Erlendur_Patursson" title="Erlendur Patursson">Erlendur Patursson</a> (1913–86), Faroese member of the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>, raised the idea of a Nordic cultural house in the Faroe Islands. A Nordic competition for architects was held in 1977, in which 158 architects participated. Winners were Ola Steen from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> and Kolbrún Ragnarsdóttir from <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>. By staying true to <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, the architects built the Nordic House to resemble an enchanted hill of <a href="/wiki/Elf" title="Elf">elves</a>. The house opened in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B3rshavn" title="Tórshavn">Tórshavn</a> in 1983. The Nordic House is a cultural organization under the Nordic Council. The Nordic House is run by a steering committee of eight, of whom three are Faroese and five from other Nordic countries. There is also a local advisory body of fifteen members, representing Faroese cultural organizations. The House is managed by a director appointed by the steering committee for a four-year term.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_food">Traditional food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Traditional food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faroese_cuisine" title="Faroese cuisine">Faroese cuisine</a></div>
<p>Traditional Faroese food is mainly based on meat, seafood and potatoes and uses few fresh vegetables. Mutton of the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_sheep" title="Faroe sheep">Faroe sheep</a> is the basis of many meals, and one of the most popular treats is <i><a href="/wiki/Skerpikj%C3%B8t" title="Skerpikjøt">skerpikjøt</a></i>, well aged, wind-dried mutton, which is quite chewy. The drying shed, known as a <i>hjallur</i>, is a standard feature in many Faroese homes, particularly in the small towns and villages. Other traditional foods are <i>ræst kjøt</i> (semi-dried mutton) and <i>ræstur fiskur</i>, matured fish. Another Faroese specialty is <i><a href="/wiki/Tv%C3%B8st_og_spik" title="Tvøst og spik">tvøst og spik</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pilot_whale" title="Pilot whale">pilot whale</a> meat and <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>. (A parallel meat/fat dish made with <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/Garnat%C3%A1lg" title="Garnatálg">garnatálg</a></i>.) Meat and blubber from a pilot whale means food for a long time. Fresh fish also features strongly in the traditional local diet, as do <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Faroese_puffin" title="Faroese puffin">Faroese puffins</a>, and their eggs. Dried fish is also commonly eaten.</p>
<p>There are two breweries in the Faroe Islands. The first brewery is called <a href="/wiki/F%C3%B6roya_Bj%C3%B3r" title="Föroya Bjór">Föroya Bjór</a> and has produced beer since 1888 with exports mainly to Iceland and Denmark. The second brewery is called Okkara Bryggjarí and was founded in 2010. A local specialty is <i>fredrikk</i>, a special brew made in <a href="/wiki/N%C3%B3lsoy" title="Nólsoy">Nólsoy</a>. Production of hard alcohol such as <a href="/wiki/Snaps" title="Snaps">snaps</a> is forbidden in the Faroe Islands, hence the Faroese <a href="/wiki/Akvavit" title="Akvavit">akvavit</a> is produced abroad.</p>
<p>Since the friendly British occupation, the Faroese have been fond of British food, in particular <a href="/wiki/Fish_and_chips" title="Fish and chips">fish and chips</a> and British-style chocolate such as <a href="/wiki/Cadbury_Dairy_Milk" title="Cadbury Dairy Milk">Cadbury Dairy Milk</a>, which is found in many of the island's shops, whereas in Denmark this is scarce.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whaling">Whaling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Whaling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Whaling in the Faroe Islands">Whaling in the Faroe Islands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG/220px-Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG/330px-Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG/440px-Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands_in_August_2012.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Boats driving a pod of pilot whales into a bay of <a href="/wiki/Su%C3%B0uroy" title="Suðuroy">Suðuroy</a> in 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are records of drive hunts in the Faroe Islands dating from 1584.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_the_Faroe_Islands" title="Whaling in the Faroe Islands">Whaling in the Faroe Islands</a> is regulated by Faroese authorities but not by the <a href="/wiki/International_Whaling_Commission" title="International Whaling Commission">International Whaling Commission</a> as there are disagreements about the Commission's legal authority to regulate <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetacean</a> hunts. Hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Long-finned_pilot_whale" title="Long-finned pilot whale">long-finned pilot whales</a> (<i>Globicephala melaena</i>) could be killed in a year, mainly during the summer. The hunts, called <i>grindadráp</i> in Faroese, are non-commercial and are organized on a community level; anyone can participate. When a whale pod by chance is spotted near land the participating hunters first surround the pilot whales with a wide semicircle of boats and then slowly and quietly begin to drive the whales towards the chosen authorised bay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Home_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Home-88">[88]</a></sup> When a pod of whales has been stranded the killing is begun. Faroese animal welfare legislation, which also applies to whaling, requires that animals are killed as quickly and with as little suffering as possible. A regulation spinal lance is used to sever the spinal cord, which also severs the major blood supply to the brain, ensuring both loss of consciousness and death within seconds. The spinal lance has been introduced as preferred standard equipment for killing pilot whales and has been shown to reduce killing time to 1–2 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Home_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Home-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>This "grindadráp" is legal and provides food for many people in the Faroe Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> However, a study has found whale meat and blubber to currently be contaminated with <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> and not recommended for human consumption, as too much may cause such adverse health effects as birth defects of the nervous system, high blood pressure, damaged immune system, increased risk for developing <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arteriosclerosis" title="Arteriosclerosis">arteriosclerosis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes_mellitus_type_2" title="Diabetes mellitus type 2">Diabetes mellitus type 2</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Therefore we recommend that adults eat no more than one to two meals a month. Women who plan to become pregnant within three months, pregnant women, and nursing women should abstain from eating pilot whale meat. Pilot whale liver and kidneys should not be eaten at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faroese_Islanders" title="Faroese Islanders">Faroese Islanders</a> consider the hunt an important part of their culture and history. <a href="/wiki/List_of_animal_rights_groups" title="List of animal rights groups">Animal rights groups</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sea_Shepherd_Conservation_Society" title="Sea Shepherd Conservation Society">Sea Shepherd Conservation Society</a>, criticize it as being cruel and unnecessary, since it in their point of view is no longer necessary as a food source for the Faroese people, while the hunters claim in return that most journalists do not exhibit sufficient knowledge of the catch methods or its economic significance.</p>
<p>The sustainability of the Faroese pilot whale hunt has been discussed, but with a long-term average catch of around 800 pilot whales on the Faroe Islands a year the hunt is not considered to have a significant impact on the pilot whale population. There are an estimated 128,000 pilot whales in the Northeast Atlantic, and Faroese whaling is therefore considered a sustainable catch by the Faroese government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Annual records of whale drives and strandings of pilot whales and other small cetaceans provide over 400 years of documentation, including statistics, and represents one of the most comprehensive historical records of wildlife utilization anywhere in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Home_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Home-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Faroe Islands have competed in every biennial <a href="/wiki/Island_Games" title="Island Games">Island Games</a> since they were established in 1985. The games were hosted by the islands in 1989 and Faroes won the <a href="/wiki/2009_Island_Games" title="2009 Island Games">Island Games in 2009</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="2479" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG/220px-P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG/330px-P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG/440px-P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P%C3%A1l_Joensen_2014.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Joensen" title="Pál Joensen">Pál Joensen</a>, Faroese swimmer</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">Football</a> is by far the biggest sports activity on the islands, with 7,000 registered players out of the whole population of 50,000. Ten football teams contest the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands_Premier_League" title="Faroe Islands Premier League">Faroe Islands Premier League</a>, currently ranked 51st by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uefa_league_coefficient" title="Uefa league coefficient">UEFA's League coefficient</a>. The Faroe Islands are a full member of <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands_national_football_team" title="Faroe Islands national football team">Faroe Islands national football team</a> competes in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Football_Championship" title="UEFA European Football Championship">UEFA European Football Championship</a> qualifiers. The country is also a full member of <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a> and therefore the Faroe Islands football team also competes in the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cup</a> qualifiers. The country won its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Faroe_Islands_national_football_team_results" title="List of Faroe Islands national football team results">first ever competitive match</a> when the team defeated <a href="/wiki/Austria_national_football_team" title="Austria national football team">Austria</a> 1–0 in a UEFA Euro 1992 qualifying. The nation's biggest success in football came in 2014 after defeating Greece 1–0, a result that was considered "the biggest shock of all time" in football<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> thanks to a 169-place distance between the teams in the <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Rankings" title="FIFA World Rankings">FIFA World Rankings</a> when the match was played. The team climbed 82 places to 105 on the FIFA ranking after the 1–0 win against Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The team went on to defeat Greece again on 13 June 2015 by a score of 2–1. On 9 July 2015 the national football team of the Faroes climbed another 28 places up on the FIFA ranking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/IHF_Emerging_Nations_Championship" title="IHF Emerging Nations Championship">IHF Emerging Nations Championship</a> has been played twice, starting in 2015, and <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands_national_handball_team" title="Faroe Islands national handball team">Faroe Islands national handball team</a> has won both editions.</p>
<p>The Faroe Islands are a full member of <a href="/wiki/FINA" title="FINA">FINA</a> and compete under their own flag at World Championships, European Championships and World Cup events. The Faroese swimmer <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Joensen" title="Pál Joensen">Pál Joensen</a> (born 1990) won a bronze medal at the <a href="/wiki/2012_FINA_World_Swimming_Championships_(25_m)" title="2012 FINA World Swimming Championships (25 m)">2012 FINA World Swimming Championships (25 m)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> and four silver medals at the European Championships (<a href="/wiki/2010_European_Aquatics_Championships" title="2010 European Aquatics Championships">2010</a>, <a href="/wiki/2013_European_Short_Course_Swimming_Championships" title="2013 European Short Course Swimming Championships">2013</a> and <a href="/wiki/2014_European_Aquatics_Championships" title="2014 European Aquatics Championships">2014</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> all medals won in the men's longest and second longest distance the 1500 and 800 metre freestyle, short and long course. The Faroe Islands compete in the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands_at_the_Paralympics" title="Faroe Islands at the Paralympics">Paralympics</a> and have won several gold, silver and bronze medals there.</p>
<p>Two Faroese athletes have competed at the Olympics, but under the <a href="/wiki/Denmark_at_the_Olympics" title="Denmark at the Olympics">Danish flag</a>, since the Olympic Committee does not allow the Faroe Islands to compete under its own flag. The two Faroese who have competed are the swimmer <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Joensen" title="Pál Joensen">Pál Joensen</a> in 2012 and the rower <a href="/wiki/Katrin_Olsen" title="Katrin Olsen">Katrin Olsen</a>. She competed at the <a href="/wiki/Rowing_at_the_2008_Summer_Olympics" title="Rowing at the 2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a> in double sculler light weight together with <a href="/wiki/Juliane_Rasmussen" title="Juliane Rasmussen">Juliane Rasmussen</a>. Another Faroese rower, who is a member of the Danish National rowing team, is <a href="/wiki/Sverri_Sandberg_Nielsen" title="Sverri Sandberg Nielsen">Sverri Sandberg Nielsen</a>, who currently competes in single sculler, heavy weight, he has also competed in double sculler. He is the current Danish record holder in the men's indoor rowing, heavy weight; he broke a nine-year-old record in January 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sportschef_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sportschef-99">[99]</a></sup> and improved it in January 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danmarkarmeistari_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danmarkarmeistari-100">[100]</a></sup> He has also competed at the <a href="/wiki/2015_World_Rowing_Championships" title="2015 World Rowing Championships">2015 World Rowing Championships</a> making it to the semifinal; he competed at the 2015 World Rowing Championship under-23 and made it to the final where he placed fourth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Rowing_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Rowing-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Faroe Islands applied to the IOC for full Faroese membership in 1984, but as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the Faroe Islands are still not a member of the IOC. The Faroes have competed under their own flag at the European Junior Championship for several years, but in 2015 the championship was held at the 1st European Games in Baku, and the Faroe Islands were not allowed to compete under the Faroese flag; they were, however, allowed to compete under the FINA flag. The Faroese prime minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaj_Leo_Holm_Johannesen" title="Kaj Leo Holm Johannesen">Kaj Leo Holm Johannesen</a> had a meeting with the IOC president Thomas Bach in Lausanne on 21 May 2015 to discuss Faroese membership in the IOC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Faroese people are very active in sports; they have domestic competitions in football, handball, volleyball, badminton, swimming, outdoor rowing (Faroese kappróður) and indoor rowing in rowing machines, horse riding, shooting, table tennis, judo, golf, tennis, archery, gymnastics, <a href="/wiki/Tour_of_Faroe_Islands" title="Tour of Faroe Islands">cycling</a>, triathlon, running, and other competitions in athletics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>During 2014 Faroe Islands was given the opportunity to compete in the Electronic Sports European Championship (ESEC) in <a href="/wiki/ESports" title="ESports">E-Sports</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> 5 players, all of Faroese nationality, faced <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> in the first round, eventually getting knocked out with a 0-2 score.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the 2016 Baku Chess Olympiad, the Faroe Islands got their first chess grandmaster. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helgi_Ziska" title="Helgi Ziska">Helgi Ziska</a> won his third GM norm and thus, won the title of chess grandmaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle_(technique)" title="Fair Isle (technique)">Fair Isle (technique)</a>.</div>
<p>Faroese handicrafts are mainly based on materials available to local villages—mainly wool. Garments include jumpers, scarves, and gloves. Faroese jumpers have distinct Nordic patterns; each village has some regional variations handed down from mother to daughter. There has recently been a strong revival of interest in Faroese knitting, with young people knitting and wearing updated versions of old patterns emphasized by strong colours and bold patterns. This appears to be a reaction to the loss of traditional lifestyles, and as a way to maintain and assert cultural tradition in a rapidly-changing society. Many young people study and move abroad, and this helps them maintain cultural links with their specific Faroese heritage.</p>
<p>There has also been a great interest in Faroese <a href="/wiki/Sweater" title="Sweater">sweaters</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> from the TV series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Killing_(Danish_TV_series)" title="The Killing (Danish TV series)">The Killing</a></i>, where the main actress (Detective Inspector Sarah Lund, played by <a href="/wiki/Sofie_Gr%C3%A5b%C3%B8l" title="Sofie Gråbøl">Sofie Gråbøl</a>) wears Faroese sweaters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lace_knitting" title="Lace knitting">Lace knitting</a> is a traditional handicraft. The most distinctive trait of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faroese_lace_shawls" title="Faroese lace shawls">Faroese lace shawls</a> is the centre-back <a href="/wiki/Gusset" title="Gusset">gusset</a> shaping. Each shawl consists of two triangular side panels, a trapezoid-shaped back gusset, an edge treatment, and usually shoulder shaping. These are worn by all generations of women, particularly as part of the traditional Faroese costume as an overgarment.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg/220px-Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg/330px-Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg/440px-Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faroese_folk_dance_club_from_vagar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Faroese folk dancers, some of them in national costume</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The traditional Faroese national dress is also a local handicraft that people spend a lot of time, money, and effort to assemble. It is worn at weddings and traditional dancing events, and on feast days. The cultural significance of the garment should not be underestimated, both as an expression of local and national identity and a passing on and reinforcing of traditional skills that bind local communities together.</p>
<p>A young Faroese person is normally handed down a set of children's Faroese clothes that have passed from generation to generation. Children are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confirmed" title="Confirmed">confirmed</a> at age 14, and normally start to collect the pieces to make an adult outfit, which is considered as a rite of passage. Traditionally the aim would have been to complete the outfit by the time a young person was ready to marry and wear the clothes at the ceremony—though it is mainly only men who do this now.</p>
<p>Each piece is intricately hand-knitted, dyed, woven or embroidered to the specifications of the wearer. For example, the man's waistcoat is put together by hand in bright blue, red or black fine wool. The front is then intricately embroidered with colourful silk threads, often by a female relative. The motifs are often local Faroese flowers or herbs. After this, a row of Faroese-made solid silver buttons are sewn on the outfit.</p>
<p>Women wear embroidered silk, cotton or wool shawls and pinafores that can take months to weave or embroider with local flora and fauna. They are also adorned with a handwoven black and red ankle-length skirt, knitted black and red jumper, a velvet belt, and black 18th century style shoes with silver buckles. The outfit is held together by a row of solid silver buttons, silver chains and locally-made silver brooches and belt buckles, often fashioned with Viking style motifs.</p>
<p>Both men's and women's national dress are extremely costly and can take many years to assemble. Women in the family often work together to assemble the outfits, including knitting the close-fitting jumpers, weaving and embroidering, sewing and assembling the national dress.</p>
<p>This tradition binds together families, passes on traditional crafts, and reinforces the Faroese culture of traditional village life in the context of a modern society.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_holidays">Public holidays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Faroe_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Public holidays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Denmark" title="Public holidays in Denmark">Public holidays in Denmark</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="429" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg/220px-F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg/330px-F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F%C3%B8roysk_fl%C3%B8gg_%C3%A1_%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ku.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The annual <i>Ólavsøka</i> parade on 28 July 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ka" title="Ólavsøka">Ólavsøka</a></i> is on 29 July; it commemorates the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Olaf" title="Saint Olaf">Saint Olaf</a>. The celebrations are held in Tórshavn, starting on the evening of the 28th and continuing until the 31st. 28 July is a half working day for the members of some of the labour unions, while Ólavsøkudagur (St Olaf's Day) on 29 July is a full holiday for most but not all union members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>The official celebration starts on the 29th, with the opening of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faroese_Parliament" title="Faroese Parliament">Faroese Parliament</a>, a custom that dates back 900 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> This begins with a service held in <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B3rshavn_Cathedral" title="Tórshavn Cathedral">Tórshavn Cathedral</a>; all members of parliament as well as civil and church officials walk to the cathedral in a procession. All of the parish ministers take turns giving the sermon. After the service, the procession returns to the parliament for the opening ceremony.</p>
<p>Other celebrations are marked by different kinds of sports competitions, the rowing competition (in Tórshavn Harbour) being the most popular, art exhibitions, pop concerts, and the famous <a href="/wiki/Faroese_dance" title="Faroese dance">Faroese dance</a> in Sjónleikarhúsið and on Vaglið outdoor singing on 29 July (continuing after midnight on 30 July). The celebrations have many facets, and only a few are mentioned here.</p>
<p>Many people also mark the occasion by wearing the national Faroese dress.</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ka" title="Ólavsøka">St.Olav's Eve</a>, 28 July (half-day holiday for some workers' unions).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/%C3%93lavs%C3%B8ka" title="Ólavsøka">St.Olav's Day</a>, 29 July (full holiday for some workers' unions).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Christmas_Eve" title="Christmas Eve">Christmas Eve</a>, 24 December.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christmas_Day" title="Christmas Day">Christmas Day</a>, 25 December.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Boxing_Day" title="Boxing Day">Boxing Day</a>, 26 December.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve" title="New Year's Eve">New Year's Eve</a>, 31 December (half-day holiday).</li>
</ul>
</div>
